10.1016/j.jmp.2023.102780
From making decisions in our daily lives to formulating policies, we frequently choose a (sub)set including several alternatives based on a preference on a finite set of alternatives and receive (or carry out) all the elements in that (sub)set. By employing cardinal utility functions, we can identify an optimal choice under budget constraints. However, if cardinal utility functions are not applicable, we must rank subsets of the alternative set using an ordinal method.

The concept of ranking sets within this framework is called ‘sets as final outcomes’ by  Barberà, Bossert, and Pattanaik (2004) . 
                         1 
                      
                      
                         1 
                         See also  Bossert (1995) ,  Fishburn (1992) ,  Gärdenfors (1976) ,  Luo, Yue, and Chen (1996) ,  Nitzan and Pattanaik (1984) , and  Roth (1985) . Two other classes have been proposed in the theories of  ranking sets of alternatives :  complete uncertainty  and  opportunity sets . In these classes, an individual is assumed to receive only one alternative from a subset chosen by the individual. See  Barberà, Bossert, and Pattanaik (2004)  for details. 
                       For situations involving a fixed quota,  
                         k 
                      , such as committee voting, ranking  
                         k 
                      -subsets is enough.  Bossert (1995)  characterised the class of lexicographic extension rules, including the leximax, leximin, median-based, and other lexicographic rules, with the fixed cardinality of subsets. If we expand the scope of this field to choice behaviours in everyday life (e.g., installing applications on our smartphones, choosing sandwich ingredients), it becomes necessary to discuss methods for ranking subsets with different cardinalities.

We employ a concept introduced by  Fishburn (1992) , using a  disjoint copy  of the alternative set to rank all subsets. Each element in the disjoint copy corresponds to each existing alternative and is referred to as a  null alternative , representing the act of ‘not choosing’ the corresponding existing alternative. Going forward, we denote the null alternative of  
                         a 
                       as  
                         
                            
                               n 
                            
                            
                               a 
                            
                         
                      .  Fishburn (1992)  provided an interpretation of the null alternatives, particularly in the context of committee voting without a quota. In this case, ‘a voter prefers  
                         
                            
                               n 
                            
                            
                               a 
                            
                         
                       to  
                         b 
                      ’ indicates that ‘it is more crucial to the voter that  
                         a 
                       not be on the committee than that  
                         b 
                       be on it’. 
                         2 
                      
                      
                         2 
                         See also  Barberà, Sonnenschein, and Zhou (1991) .

Lexicographic ranking of all subsets can be achieved by creating each  transformed subset , which incorporates both the existing alternatives and the null alternatives of other existing alternatives. The elements of every transformed subset are subsequently rearranged in descending order. Under the leximax (alternatively, leximin) rule, any two subsets are ranked by comparing the same-ranked (null) alternatives in the corresponding two transformed subsets from top to bottom (alternatively, bottom to top). In the following example, we will present a method for ordering transformed subsets.

Employing weak orders on the existing and null alternatives without any additional properties leads to a breach of  extensibility  by the leximax and leximin rules. Extensibility requires that the preference order of  
                         a 
                       and  
                         b 
                       should be equivalent to that of the sets  
                         
                            { 
                            a 
                            } 
                         
                       and  
                         
                            { 
                            b 
                            } 
                         
                      . 
                         3 
                      
                      
                         3 
                         
                            Extensibility  has been called the  congruence condition  in  Fishburn (1992)  or an  extension rule  in related fields, such as  complete uncertainty  and  opportunity sets  (see ( Barberà et al., 2004 )). 
                       Thus, the objective of this study is to find sufficient conditions for weak orders to ensure that the leximax and leximin rules on the power set satisfy extensibility. 
                         4 
                      
                      
                         4 
                         We do not research a median-based rule such as  Nitzan and Pattanaik (1984)  rule because we need an additional operation to rank the pairs of both the outsides of the medians. Thus, such a rule is excluded from this study.

Fishburn (1992)  introduced  self-reflecting , which requires that (i)  
                         
                            a 
                            ≿ 
                            b 
                         
                       if and only if  
                         
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  b 
                               
                            
                            ≿ 
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  a 
                               
                            
                         
                      , (ii)  
                         
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  a 
                               
                            
                            ≿ 
                            b 
                         
                       if and only if  
                         
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  b 
                               
                            
                            ≿ 
                            a 
                         
                      , and (iii)  
                         
                            a 
                            ≿ 
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  b 
                               
                            
                         
                       if and only if  
                         
                            b 
                            ≿ 
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  a 
                               
                            
                         
                      . Here, we suppose that  
                         a 
                       and  
                         b 
                       are existing alternatives. If a weak order satisfies self-reflecting, it is called a  signed order .  Luo et al. (1996)  characterised the lexicographic extension rule with signed orders. However, self-reflecting restricts the scope of considerable situations as it does not permit null alternatives to be indifferent if the existing alternatives are not indifferent.

We then introduce the following two new properties. The first property is  semi-inversion desirability , which requires that if  
                         
                            a 
                            ≿ 
                            b 
                         
                      , then  
                         
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  b 
                               
                            
                            ≿ 
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  a 
                               
                            
                         
                      . The second property is  consistent desirability , which requires that if  
                         
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  a 
                               
                            
                            ≻ 
                            b 
                         
                      , then  
                         
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  b 
                               
                            
                            ≿ 
                            a 
                         
                      , and if  
                         
                            a 
                            ≻ 
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  b 
                               
                            
                         
                      , then  
                         
                            b 
                            ≿ 
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  a 
                               
                            
                         
                      .

The size of a preference domain and the strength of restrictions imposed on the domain are interdependent. Signed orders provide a way to reduce the preference domain and to apply preferences to traditional choice or utility models. Furthermore,  Fishburn (2001)  justified using linear signed orders based on the tradition of continuous random utility models for probabilistic choice and their relative simplicity. While Fishburn’s ( Fishburn, 2001 ) justification is applicable to committee elections, where many voting rules require a profile of linear preferences, using signed orders may unreasonably constrain preference domains in daily situations.

In the context of lexicographic rules, we show that semi-inversion desirability implies extensibility, and self-reflecting implies both semi-inversion desirability and consistent desirability. Furthermore, we show that the leximax and leximin rules with weak orders satisfying semi-inversion desirability and consistent desirability are not equivalent, but the leximax and leximin extension rules with signed orders are equivalent.

The remainder of this paper is structured as follows. Section  2  reports our notations and definitions. Section  3  defines the leximax and leximin rules. Section  4  introduces several sufficient conditions for making the leximax and leximin rules satisfy extensibility and discusses the need for semi-inversion desirability. Section  5  shows that semi-inversion desirability is a weaker sufficient condition for making the leximax and leximin rules satisfy extensibility compared to self-reflecting. Section  6  discusses (i) the need for consistent desirability and (ii) the differences between signed orders and weak orders satisfying semi-inversion desirability and consistent desirability. Finally, Section  7  provides concluding remarks and future research directions.

Considering only subsets with fixed cardinality, the leximax and leximin rules satisfy EX.  Bossert (1995)  analysed a lexicographic method to rank any two  
                         k 
                      –subsets of  
                         X 
                      , where  
                         
                            k 
                            < 
                            # 
                            X 
                         
                      . Each individual arranges all alternatives in any two  
                         k 
                      -subsets in descending order and compares the same-ranked alternatives from top to bottom (or bottom to top). She then constructs her preferences on  
                         
                            
                               2 
                            
                            
                               X 
                            
                         
                      . We can apply the lexicographic method to committee voting with a fixed quota,  
                         k 
                      . In this framework, we do not need any null alternative.

However, we cannot rank subsets with different cardinalities using Bossert’s ( Bossert, 1995 ) method. Furthermore, the following example shows that we cannot distinguish a better lexicographic rule (or have to change a construction rule for each pair of subsets) by using Bossert’s ( Bossert, 1995 ) method:

Roth (1985)  introduced the concept of empty slots,

Fishburn (1992, 2001)  proposed a weak order satisfying  self-reflecting :

Self-reflecting (SR) 
                            
                               :  
                                     
                                        ∀ 
                                        a 
                                        , 
                                        b 
                                        ∈ 
                                        X 
                                        ∪ 
                                        N 
                                     
                                  ,  
                                     
                                        a 
                                        ≿

                                              a

                                  .

:  
                                     
                                        ∀ 
                                        a 
                                        , 
                                        b 
                                        ∈ 
                                        X 
                                        ∪ 
                                        N 
                                     
                                  ,  
                                     
                                        a 
                                        ≿ 
                                        b 
                                        ⇔

                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                        ≿ 
                                        
                                           
                                              n 
                                           
                                           
                                              b 
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                  .

SR requires that a preference on any two (existing or null) alternatives and that on the corresponding (null or existing) alternatives are opposite.

Fishburn (1992, 2001)  called a weak order satisfying SR a ‘signed order’.  Fishburn (2001)  and  Fiorini and Fishburn (2003)  characterised the set of signed orders by two approaches: (i) a polyhedron and (ii) binary choice probabilities and random preference orders. 
                            7 
                         
                         
                            7 
                            Our study focuses on sufficient conditions weaker than SR for making  
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                     
                                        l 
                                        m 
                                        a 
                                        x 
                                     
                                  
                                and  
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                     
                                        l 
                                        m 
                                        i 
                                        n 
                                     
                                  
                                satisfy EX.  Fishburn (2001)  mentioned the lexicographic rules only once by referring to  Luo et al. (1996) , and  Fiorini and Fishburn (2003)  did not mention the topic. These references focused on analyses of linear signed orders. Thus, we do not introduce the representations of weak signed orders or other weak orders on  
                                  
                                     X 
                                     ∪ 
                                     N 
                                  
                                using polytopes. Furthermore,   Fishburn (2001)  and  Fiorini and Fishburn (2003)  studied  linear  signed orders since the number of weak signed orders is enormous. However, when we decide a preference on  
                                  
                                     X 
                                     ∪ 
                                     N 
                                  
                               , we do not need to check all weak signed orders as search programmes. Since it is possible to have indifference orders on some alternatives in  
                                  
                                     X 
                                     ∪ 
                                     N 
                                  
                               , considering weak orders would be meaningful.

However,  Fishburn (1992, 2001)  do not discuss how strong SR is as an assumption for  
                            ≿ 
                          when we consider making  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  m 
                                  a 
                                  x 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  m 
                                  i 
                                  n 
                               
                            
                          satisfy EX. Thus, we will discuss the strength of SR and find some weaker properties for  
                            ≿ 
                          on  
                            
                               X 
                               ∪ 
                               N 
                            
                          to obtain reasonable lexicographic weak extension rules.

We introduce a property called  semi-inversion desirability  (SID) for  
                            ≿ 
                         . SID requires that, for any two existing alternatives  
                            
                               a 
                               , 
                               b 
                               ∈ 
                               X 
                            
                         , if  
                            a 
                          is at least as good as  
                            b 
                         , then  
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  b 
                               
                            
                          is at least as good as  
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  a 
                               
                            
                         .

Semi-inversion desirability (SID) 
                            
                               :  
                                     
                                        ∀ 
                                        a 
                                        , 
                                        b 
                                        ∈ 
                                        X 
                                     
                                  ,  
                                     
                                        a 
                                        ≿ 
                                        b 
                                        ⇒ 
                                        
                                           
                                              n 
                                           
                                           
                                              b 
                                           
                                        
                                        ≿ 
                                        
                                           
                                              n 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                  .

:  
                                     
                                        ∀ 
                                        a 
                                        , 
                                        b 
                                        ∈ 
                                        X 
                                     
                                  ,  
                                     
                                        a 
                                        ≿ 
                                        b 
                                        ⇒ 
                                        
                                           
                                              n 
                                           
                                           
                                              b 
                                           
                                        
                                        ≿ 
                                        
                                           
                                              n 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                  .

We call weak orders satisfying SID ‘SID-weak orders’.

If we require the opposite direction of SID as SR, and any two existing alternatives are strictly ordered, then the corresponding null alternatives cannot be indifferent. The following example illustrates this point:

From  Proposition 2 ,  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  m 
                                  a 
                                  x 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  m 
                                  i 
                                  n 
                               
                            
                          become similar using SICD-weak orders. We then have the question: when do  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  m 
                                  a 
                                  x 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  m 
                                  i 
                                  n 
                               
                            
                          produce the same preference order of subsets?

To start with the answer, we should note that employing signed orders renders the leximax and leximin rules equivalent ( Proposition 4  will show this result). However, this finding does not imply that the lexicographic rule with a signed order is superior to the leximax or leximin rule with a SICD-weak order. If an individual perceives SR to be overly restrictive, then SICD-weak orders may be more reasonable, and she must establish a priority order of the leximax and leximin criteria. Conversely, if an individual deems SICD-weak orders non-intuitive, then signed orders may be more fitting for her.

Proposition 3  shows that the outcomes of  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  m 
                                  a 
                                  x 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  l 
                                  m 
                                  i 
                                  n 
                               
                            
                          with SICD-weak orders are not always equivalent.  Example 6  proves  Proposition 3 .

PS requires that each alternative and its null alternative are located at symmetric positions and that preference orders are symmetric. For example, if  
                            
                               X 
                               =

                               X

                               N

                               ≻ 
                               
                                  
                                     n

                         . Thus, we need only a preference order of

Lemma 1  shows that SR and PS are equivalent.

Lemma 1 
                            
                               For any  
                               
                                  
                                     ≿ 
                                     ∈ 
                                     ℛ 
                                  
                               
                               ,  
                               
                                  ≿ 
                                
                               satisfies SR if and only if it satisfies PS.

For any  
                               
                                  
                                     ≿ 
                                     ∈

Conversely, if an individual finds that SR is intuitive, and SICD does not guarantee rational choices, a signed order is the most reasonable for her. Furthermore, she evaluates each null alternative according to opportunity costs, such as ‘if an alternative is very desirable, the corresponding null alternative is very undesirable’. That is, a signed order is close to a cardinal utility.

We discuss the properties of weak orders on the set of alternatives and null alternatives for making the leximax and leximin rules satisfy EX: that is, NI, SR, SID, and CD. We find that (i) NI, SR, or SID implies EX, (ii) CD does not imply EX, (iii) SR implies both SID and CD, and (iv) the leximax and leximin rules, when used with signed orders, are equivalent.

When an individual considers some null alternatives to be indifferent when the corresponding alternatives are not indifferent, SID and CD are more appropriate properties than SR. Conversely, when an individual interprets null alternatives based on opportunity costs, SR is more suitable than SID and CD.

Finally, we discuss an extension of this study. Ordinal ranking rules on the power set, such as the lexicographic rules, would help individuals choose (sub)sets. To improve the accuracy of these rules, we should consider ‘compatibility within alternatives’. By considering the compatibility, ordinal extension rules can more closely approximate ranking rules using cardinal utilities. After this extension, the lexicographic extension rules on the power set will be helpful to propose discrete optimisations in individual decision-making, as well as for new multi-winner voting systems and other applications.
